# Stage 4 of 19: post-human-normalize
# Book: chaoskampf
# Generated: 2026-04-28T17:46:21Z

# Governing file: scope.md

```
# Scope: Chaoskampf Across Cultures

## Book type
**Cross-cultural comparison.** Unlike the other books in this project (one-culture-per-book), this book's subject is a *motif family* — the combat-with-chaos myth — traced across ~15 ancient traditions. The comparison is the point, not a capstone. The `comparative` stage of the pipeline is therefore the engine of the book rather than the final chapter.

## Subject definition
"Chaoskampf" here means: a narrative in which a sovereign or would-be sovereign deity (or heroic champion) defeats a serpentine / monstrous adversary, and that combat does one or more of the following order-making kinds of work:
1. **Cosmogonic** — the cosmos is established from or after the combat (e.g., Marduk/Tiamat, Ninurta/Asag, Gonggong).
2. **Kingship / sovereignty** — the victor's rule over gods and/or humans is established (Baal/Yamm, Zeus/Typhon, Indra/Vṛtra).
3. **Cyclical maintenance** — the combat is re-enacted, preserving an order that is always under threat (Ra/Apep, Illuyanka in the *purulli* festival).
4. **Eschatological** — the combat closes cosmic time (Thor/Jörmungandr at Ragnarök, Rev 12, Frashokereti).

This working definition is operational, not doctrinal. The book will explicitly flag that the category "Chaoskampf" is contested in current scholarship (see §"Terminological position" below).

## Terminological position
The term *Chaoskampf* was coined by Hermann Gunkel (*Schöpfung und Chaos in Urzeit und Endzeit*, 1895). It has been critiqued in successive waves, most consequentially in:
- *Creation and Chaos: A Reconsideration of Hermann Gunkel's Chaoskampf Hypothesis*, ed. JoAnn Scurlock & Richard Beal (Eisenbrauns, 2013).
- Debra Scoggins Ballentine, *The Conflict Myth and the Biblical Tradition* (OUP, 2015), who argues "chaos" is the wrong word — adversaries are better read as agents of an alternative divine power structure.

The book will include a **dedicated introductory chapter** titled provisionally "What Chaoskampf is, and why the word is contested." The term is retained because it is what readers will search for and what scholars still (if uneasily) use, but the book acknowledges the critique and adopts "combat myth" as an interchangeable term where precision matters.

## Period
- Earliest in-scope primary source: Sumerian *Lugal-e* (Ninurta vs. Asag), Old Babylonian copies of Ur III originals — c. 2000 BCE.
- Latest in-scope primary source: Zoroastrian *Bundahišn*, late Sasanian / early Islamic redaction — c. 8th–9th c. CE.
- Secondary (scholarly) literature is contemporary (1895–present).

Post-medieval reworkings (Milton's Satan, Tolkien's Smaug, etc.) are explicitly **out of scope as primary material** but may appear briefly in the concluding chapter as reception history.

## Languages of primary sources
Sumerian, Akkadian, Hurrian, Hittite, Ugaritic, Biblical Hebrew, Koine Greek, Classical Greek, Latin, Vedic Sanskrit, Classical Sanskrit, Avestan, Middle Persian (Pahlavi), Old Egyptian (and later Egyptian through Demotic), Old Norse (Eddic + skaldic), Old English, Classical Nahuatl, Quechua, Classical Chinese, Old Japanese (*man'yōgana*), Classical Armenian, Old Church Slavonic / later East Slavic, Old Irish, Middle Welsh, Old Finnish (Kalevala-era runes), and oral-tradition records in Nyanga, Soninke, Visayan/Bikol, and Mapudungun.

The book is **translation-mediated**. The author does not read most of these languages. The source-whitelist strategy (see §"Source strategy") requires two things for each language: (1) a single scholar-standard translation that will be cited first, and (2) at least one secondary-scholarship cross-check. Where a translation is disputed, the chapter will flag the variant and footnote both readings.

## In-scope catalog

**CORE (15 stories)** — full chapters, each treating the primary text(s), the combat narrative in plain-English retelling, and the scholarly debate. See `catalog.yaml` for the complete list with key scholars.

**RELATED (~30 stories)** — shorter treatments. See `catalog.yaml`. Two options for format (see DECIDE_SCOPE.md §1):
- (A) Each gets an abbreviated chapter (2–5 pages).
- (B) Grouped into "family" chapters by tradition or subtype (e.g., "Greek dragon-slayings after Zeus-Typhon"; "Indo-European reflexes of Watkins's *HERO SLAY SERPENT*"; "Mesopotamian minor combats").

**PROVISIONAL / Methodological** — Rainbow Serpent as the dedicated critique case; Yu the Great as a sidebar contrasting hydraulic-ordering with combat-ordering.

**CONTRASTIVE / Inversion** — dedicated chapters for:
- **The inversion (Bida)** — where slaying the serpent *destroys* prosperity rather than establishing it.
- **The refusal (Nanabozho / Mishipeshu)** — where area specialists explicitly reject the chaos-vs-order mapping, and the Thunderer / Underwater-manitouk dialectic is necessary rather than adversarial.

**EXCLUDED** — see `catalog.yaml` §"exclude".

## Inclusion / exclusion criteria (the operational test)
A story is in CORE if it meets **all** of:
1. The adversary is a serpentine / monstrous figure.
2. The combat is explicitly attested in a primary source (not a modern reconstruction alone).
3. The combat does cosmogonic, kingship, cyclical, or eschatological order-making work as identified by *area specialists*, not only by pattern-matching comparativists.
4. The scholarly corpus treating it is robust enough that we can cite multiple independent area-specialist readings.

A story is RELATED if it meets (1)–(2) but (3) is weaker, or if (3) holds only under a comparativist reading that area specialists qualify. Apollo/Python and Susanoo/Orochi were moved here for this reason.

A story is PROVISIONAL / CRITIQUE if area specialists explicitly reject the chaoskampf framing (Rainbow Serpent, Yu/Xiangliu), or if the combat is absent or inverted (Nanabozho, Bida handled in dedicated chapters with the right framing).

## Appropriation protocol
Given scholarly warnings about forcing Near Eastern / Indo-European templates onto non-Eurasian traditions (explicitly flagged for Rainbow Serpent, Nanabozho, Mwindo in the research notes), every RELATED chapter on a non-Eurasian tradition must:
1. Cite at least two area specialists **by name** in the first factual pass.
2. Record any specialist objection to the chaoskampf framing in an `[APPROPRIATION-FLAG:]` marker, which the `comparative` chapter must address rather than paper over.
3. Open with the tradition's own framing (*maʿat*/*isfet*, *vṛ-*/"obstruction," *wak'a* succession, etc.) before invoking the comparative category.

## Excluded adjacent motifs (contamination risks)
A cross-cultural book does not exclude neighboring *cultures* (that would defeat its purpose); it excludes *adjacent motifs* that readers and lazy comparativists conflate with chaoskampf. The book explicitly excludes:
1. **Dismemberment cosmogonies without combat** (Ymir, Puruṣa, Pangu, arguably Cipactli) — these are order-making but not combat-order-making. Cipactli is kept in RELATED precisely to mark this boundary, not ignore it.
2. **Hero-initiation monster fights** (Fionn vs. Aillen, most of Herakles's non-Hydra labours outside the serpent-combat corpus) — order-making for the hero, not for the cosmos.
3. **Inter-species enmity / folkloric monsters** (Garuḍa vs. nāgas, Welsh Afanc, Sedna) — no cosmogonic or sovereignty function.
4. **Trickster-demiurge tales without serpent-adversary** (Māui fishing the land).
5. **Storm-god theophanies without a dragon combat** (most Yoruba Ṣàngó material, Korean foundation myths).
6. **Post-medieval literary reworkings** as primary sources (see §"Period").

## Reconstruction policy
Because every chapter is a different tradition with a different textual situation, reconstruction is handled **per chapter** in the brief, not book-globally. The governing principles are:
1. **No cross-tradition reconstruction.** A gap in the *Hymiskviða* may not be filled from the *Baal Cycle*, even if structurally analogous.
2. **In-tradition reconstruction** must specify the donor source and period-proximity tolerance (e.g., "any Rigvedic hymn; never post-Vedic").
3. All reconstructions carry `[RECONSTRUCTION:]` markers naming the donor source.
4. For fragmentary cases (*Marduk vs. Labbu*, *Song of Hedammu*, some vishap material), the chapter explicitly states the fragmentary status and presents both the securely attested material and the reconstructed narrative separately.

## Variant presentation policy
- When one variant dominates a tradition's scholarly corpus (e.g., Snorri's version of the Thor fishing episode), it is presented in the body with minor variants in footnotes.
- When variants are roughly co-equal within a tradition (the two Illuyanka versions on the same tablet group; the Homeric Hymn vs. Ovidian Apollo/Python), they are presented side by side with source attributions.
- Cross-tradition comparison is never performed inline in a story-chapter; it is confined to the `comparative` material (see §"Comparative pipeline shape").

## Book structure (locked 2026-04-23)

### Part I — story chapters, geographic clusters
Part I is organised geographically, in a sequence that respects the known Near-Eastern → Mediterranean transmission chain (West, Burkert, López-Ruiz) and then moves outward. Within each cluster, CORE stories get full chapters; closely related RELATED stories are bundled into "family" chapters rather than spun out as individual mini-chapters.

Provisional geographic clusters:
1. Mesopotamia — Ninurta/Asag, Ninurta/Anzû, Marduk/Tiamat (full chapters each); family chapter on minor Mesopotamian combats (Marduk/Labbu).
2. Anatolia & Levant — Kumarbi Cycle / Ullikummi, Illuyanka, Baal/Yamm-Lotan (full chapters each).
3. Hebrew Bible & Early Christianity — Yahweh vs. Leviathan/Rahab (full); Revelation 12 Red Dragon (full); family chapter on Christian reception (St. George / Michael lineage, Ogden 2021).
4. Greece & Rome — Zeus vs. Typhon (full); family chapter "Greek dragon-combats after Zeus-Typhon" (Apollo/Python, Herakles/Hydra-Ladon-Geryon, Perseus/Ketos, Kadmos, Bellerophon, Cacus).
5. Iran — Thraētaona vs. Aži Dahāka (full); Frashokereti as its eschatological pendant.
6. India — Indra vs. Vṛtra (full); family chapter on Vedic and post-Vedic reflexes (Trita Āptya; Krishna/Kāliya as bhakti reworking).
7. Egypt — Ra vs. Apep (full); shorter treatment of Horus vs. Seth as the debated adjacent case.
8. Norse, Germanic, and IE reflexes — Thor vs. Jörmungandr (full); family chapter "Indo-European reflexes of HERO SLAY SERPENT" (Sigurd/Fáfnir, Beowulf, Lugh/Balor, Welsh Red vs. White Dragon, Batraz, Vahagn/Vishaps); family chapter on Slavic-Baltic combat (Perun/Veles reconstruction, Dobrynya byliny).
9. East Asia — Gonggong (full); family chapter on other Chinese combats (Hou Yi); Susanoo vs. Orochi (full chapter, with explicit framing that the chaoskampf label is applied from outside, per Naumann / Isomae / Como). Yu the Great appears as a sidebar contrasting hydraulic-ordering with combat-ordering, not as a chaoskampf.
10. Americas — Huitzilopochtli / Coatepec (full); family chapter on other Mesoamerican combats (Cipactli as dismemberment-boundary; Quetzalcoatl vs. Tezcatlipoca); Andean Pariacaca vs. amaru; Mapuche Kai-Kai vs. Tren-Tren.
11. Africa — Bida and the founding of Wagadu (full chapter; the inversion anchor); Mwindo vs. Kirimu (contrastive case: the hero is punished for the killing).
12. Southeast Asia & Oceania — Bakunawa. (Rainbow Serpent is held for the Part II critique chapter, not placed geographically.)
13. Finnic — Väinämöinen vs. Iku-Turso (RELATED transformed-variant chapter).
14. North America (contrastive) — Nanabozho and Mishipeshu: the refusal — the chapter where area specialists (Hallowell, Johnston, T. Smith, Vizenor) are foregrounded and the chaoskampf framing is explicitly declined.

### Part II — thematic comparative chapters
Part II is where the comparative work happens. Load-bearing (confirmed):
- **C1. Combat as cosmogony** — Marduk, Ninurta/Asag, Gonggong, Huitzilopochtli.
- **C2. Combat as sovereignty** — Baal/Yamm, Zeus/Typhon, Indra/Vṛtra, Thraētaona, Kumarbi/Ullikummi, Ninurta/Anzû.
- **C3. Cyclical maintenance** — Ra/Apep, Illuyanka.
- **C4. Eschatological displacement** — Thor/Jörmungandr, Rev 12, Frashokereti.

Recommended additional comparative chapters (confirmed at scope-lock):
- **C5. The Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT formula** — Watkins's argument from Indra through Sigurd to Beowulf.
- **C6. The Near Eastern → Mediterranean transmission** — West / Burkert / López-Ruiz: Kumarbi → Hesiod; Baal/Yamm → Typhonomachy.
- **C7. The inversion problem** — Bida anchor; the ambiguous status of Sigurd's slaying.
- **C8. The refusal and the appropriation problem** — Nanabozho, Mwindo, Rainbow Serpent, and how not to flatten traditions into chaoskampf.

### Front matter, back matter, and appendices
- **Pre-chapter (00A-introduction)**: "What Chaoskampf is, and why the word is contested." Front-loaded framing of the category, Gunkel's 1895 thesis, the 20th/21st-century critiques (Scurlock & Beal, Ballentine), and the book's operational definition (§"Subject definition").
- **Methods sidebar** between Part I and Part II: tier-ranking method, appropriation protocol, area-specialist citation policy.
- **Reception coda** (final chapter, ~20–25 pages): traces the afterlife of the imagery in Milton's Satan, Christian iconography of St. Michael / St. George, Tolkien's dragons (and the 1936 Beowulf essay), modern kaiju (Godzilla and the Orochi lineage), and the continuing use of "chaos vs. order" rhetoric in contemporary religious interpretation. Explicitly *not* classified as chaoskampf; this is reception, not primary material.
- **Adversaries + champions appendix** (replaces the single-culture "character appendix"): one entry per monster (Tiamat, Yamm, Lôtan, Leviathan, Rahab, Vṛtra, Aži Dahāka, Apep, Jörmungandr, Typhon, Illuyanka, Ullikummi, Hedammu, the Red Dragon, Coyolxauhqui, etc.) plus one entry per champion. This is load-bearing because the cross-cultural comparisons happen across *figures*, not plots.
- **Bibliography**: preliminary estimate 150–250 entries.

### Terminology default (locked)
Headings and catalog use "Chaoskampf" (reader expectation, search-term coverage). Body prose uses "combat myth" as the default, reserving "Chaoskampf" for quotations, discussions of Gunkel's term, and the Part I and Part II passages where the specificity matters. This lets the book engage the Ballentine critique substantively rather than tokenistically.

### Deferred decisions
- Spanish edition (deferred at scope-lock; no effect on Part I/II work, but glossary-lock will need to re-check if/when this is decided).
- Cover image (deferred; decide before `format-finalize`).

## Book register
Asimov-style retelling. Narrative prose in the body. Citations in AsciiDoc footnotes. Inferences, lacunae, reconstructions, appropriation warnings, and variants flagged inline with explicit markers. Plain-English register — no unexplained technical terms on first use; every ancient-language term glossed at first occurrence and collected in the glossary.

## Meta
- Book slug: `chaoskampf`
- Version: scope v1 — draft of 2026-04-23
- Author: Jose Blanca
- Open questions before scope-lock closes: see `DECIDE_SCOPE.md`
```

```
blacklist:
  - pattern: "theoi.com"
    reason: "Unattributed compilations; cite only the ancient sources it paraphrases, never theoi.com itself."
  - pattern: "sacred-texts.com"
    reason: "Archive of public-domain 19th-century translations, often superseded. Use only when a 19th-c. translation is itself the object of discussion."
  - pattern: "Wikipedia citation as sole source"
    reason: "Wikipedia may be used for orientation and for locating primary references; never as a terminal citation."
  - pattern: "ancient-origins.net"
    reason: "Sensationalist popularization. Never cite."
  - pattern: "New Age / 'universal mythology' popularizations (Campbell's *Masks of God* as primary, Graves's *Greek Myths*, Pinsent, Cotterell popular compilations)"
    reason: "Not peer-reviewed; often conflates traditions. Campbell and Graves may appear as reception/history-of-interpretation but never as factual anchors."

triangulation_databases:
  - id: etcsl
    url: "https://etcsl.orinst.ox.ac.uk/"
    covers: "Sumerian literary texts (Lugal-e, Sumerian flood story, Sumerian Anzu background)."
  - id: cdli
    url: "https://cdli.ucla.edu/"
    covers: "Cuneiform tablet database; verify specific tablet numbers."
  - id: oracc
    url: "https://oracc.museum.upenn.edu/"
    covers: "Open Richly Annotated Cuneiform Corpus — composite normalized texts, incl. Enūma Eliš, Anzu Epic."
  - id: ctr-ugaritic
    url: "https://www.zotero.org/groups/ugaritic/"
    covers: "Ugaritic text references; cross-check KTU/CAT numbering."
  - id: perseus
    url: "http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/"
    covers: "Greek and Latin primary texts — Hesiod, Apollodorus, Nonnus, Ovid, Virgil."
  - id: titus
    url: "https://titus.uni-frankfurt.de/"
    covers: "Indo-European text archive — Vedic, Avestan, Hittite."
  - id: kotenbunko
    url: "https://www.nijl.ac.jp/"
    covers: "Japanese classical texts (Kojiki, Nihon Shoki, Fudoki). Japanese-language; use with Philippi/Aston translations."
  - id: worldcat
    url: "https://www.worldcat.org/"
    covers: "Catalog lookup for verifying edition/ISBN details of hard-to-find works (van Dijk 1983, Annus 2001)."

notes:
  scope: |
    This registry is deliberately INCOMPLETE at scope-lock. It covers:
    (a) the comparative / theoretical spine that every chapter must engage with;
    (b) one anchor critical edition per CORE story;
    (c) tradition-specific anchors for the most load-bearing RELATED and contrastive entries.
    Additional per-story sources — alternative translations, specialist journal articles,
    monographs on individual combats — will accrue at the chapter-briefs stage.
    The `inventory-audit` stage is expected to flag additional required entries.
```

---

# Governing file: glossary.yaml

```
meta:
  stage: glossary-lock
  version: v1-locked
  status: user-confirmed
  date: 2026-04-24
  note: "User-confirmed on 2026-04-24. All DECIDE_GLOSSARY.md items resolved. Changes from v1-draft: (3) Yahweh replaces YHWH as primary form; (7) wak'a replaces huaca; (13) tehôm kept italicised throughout body prose rather than switching to 'the deep'; (15) 'the adversary' added as the neutral category term, with 'the dragon' reserved for morphologically draconic figures and 'chaos-figure' banned. All other draft choices confirmed. Meta-diacritics policy: full scholarly diacritics across the glossary (option A)."
  agent_model: "claude-opus-4-7[1m]"

terms:

  # ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
  # CATEGORY TERMS — the vocabulary of the book itself
  # ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

  - native: "Chaoskampf"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "Chaoskampf", source: "Gunkel 1895; still standard" }
      - { form: "combat myth", source: "Forsyth 1987; Yarbro Collins 1976; Ballentine 2015" }
      - { form: "conflict myth", source: "Ballentine 2015 preferred term" }
    chosen: "Chaoskampf (in headings and as technical term); 'combat myth' in body prose"
    rationale: "Scope.md §'Terminology default (locked)': Chaoskampf in headings/catalog; combat myth as default in body; reserves Chaoskampf for quotations and Gunkel discussion."
    first_mention_gloss: "the scholarly category, coined by Hermann Gunkel in 1895, for narratives in which a sovereign or champion deity defeats a monstrous adversary to establish or preserve cosmic order"
    never_use: ["dragon myth (too narrow)", "dragon-slaying myth (misses the cosmogonic function)"]

  - native: "cosmogonic combat"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "cosmogonic combat", source: "book-internal" }
      - { form: "cosmogonic chaoskampf", source: "Scurlock & Beal 2013" }
      - { form: "combat-cosmogony", source: "Forsyth 1987" }
    chosen: "cosmogonic combat"
    rationale: "Clearest plain-English form; the category is one of four subtypes the book defines."
    first_mention_gloss: "a combat myth in which the cosmos itself is established out of or immediately after the combat — for example, Marduk building sky and earth from Tiamat's body"
    never_use: []

  - native: "sovereignty combat"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "sovereignty combat", source: "book-internal; cf. Lincoln 2012" }
      - { form: "kingship combat", source: "Forsyth 1987" }
    chosen: "sovereignty combat"
    rationale: "Matches scope.md subtype naming; 'sovereignty' is the category that captures Baal/Yamm, Zeus/Typhon, Indra/Vṛtra, Ninurta/Anzû."
    first_mention_gloss: "a combat myth in which the victor's rule over gods and humans is established or secured by the combat"
    never_use: []

  - native: "cyclical-maintenance combat"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "cyclical-maintenance combat", source: "book-internal" }
      - { form: "recurrent combat", source: "Assmann 2001 on maʿat" }
    chosen: "cyclical-maintenance combat"
    rationale: "Captures the Ra/Apep and Illuyanka/purulli subtype; the combat is re-enacted rather than once-and-done."
    first_mention_gloss: "a combat myth that is re-enacted on a recurring schedule (daily, annually, calendrically) to preserve an order that is always under threat"
    never_use: []

  - native: "eschatological combat"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "eschatological combat", source: "book-internal; cf. Yarbro Collins 1976" }
      - { form: "end-time combat", source: "popular usage" }
    chosen: "eschatological combat"
    rationale: "Standard scholarly term; captures Ragnarök, Rev 12, Frashokereti."
    first_mention_gloss: "a combat myth that closes cosmic time — the final combat that ends the present age"
    never_use: []

  - native: "theogony / theogonic"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "theogony", source: "Hesiod title; standard scholarly use" }
      - { form: "divine genealogy", source: "popularizing" }
    chosen: "theogony / theogonic"
    rationale: "Standard; used in West 1966, West 1997, Hoffner 1998 for the Kumarbi Cycle."
    first_mention_gloss: "a narrative of the generation of the gods; the theogonic 'kingship in heaven' pattern tracks successions of divine rulers (Alalu → Anu → Kumarbi → Teshub)"
    never_use: []

  - native: "cosmogony / cosmogonic"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "cosmogony", source: "standard scholarly" }
      - { form: "creation myth", source: "popular" }
    chosen: "cosmogony / cosmogonic"
    rationale: "Precision — creation-myth is broader and includes dismemberment-without-combat cases that the book explicitly separates."
    first_mention_gloss: "a narrative of the origin of the ordered cosmos"
    never_use: ["creation myth (used only informally)"]

  - native: "reception / reception history"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "reception", source: "standard in biblical and classical studies (Ogden 2013)" }
      - { form: "Nachleben", source: "German scholarly usage" }
      - { form: "afterlife", source: "popular" }
    chosen: "reception"
    rationale: "Discipline-standard; understood by lay readers; scope.md §'Period' marks the reception coda explicitly as reception, not primary."
    first_mention_gloss: "the afterlife of an image, narrative, or motif in later traditions (e.g., Leviathan reworked in Milton, Tolkien's dragons, kaiju cinema)"
    never_use: []

  - native: "appropriation"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "appropriation", source: "current critical scholarship; scope.md §'Appropriation protocol'" }
      - { form: "cultural appropriation", source: "popular" }
    chosen: "appropriation"
    rationale: "Used in scope.md and in the Merlan 1998 / Hallowell 1960 literature without 'cultural' prefix; the prefix is redundant in context."
    first_mention_gloss: "the distortion that occurs when a comparative category developed in one tradition is imposed on another whose own conceptual framework rejects the category"
    never_use: []

  - native: "comparativist / comparative mythology"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "comparativist", source: "Lincoln 2012; contemporary critical scholarship" }
      - { form: "comparative mythologist", source: "older scholarship" }
    chosen: "comparativist"
    rationale: "The book uses 'comparativist' with its critical edge; 'comparative mythologist' would sound neutral where the book's critique is sharper."
    first_mention_gloss: "a scholar who compares myths across traditions for shared patterns; the book engages comparativism as a method while flagging its historical entanglement with universalising and colonial projects"
    never_use: []

  - native: "area specialist"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "area specialist", source: "scope.md §'Appropriation protocol'; internal term" }
      - { form: "area expert", source: "informal" }
    chosen: "area specialist"
    rationale: "Locked by scope.md; the contrast area-specialist / comparativist is load-bearing throughout."
    first_mention_gloss: "a scholar whose primary expertise is a single tradition (language, texts, ritual context); the book's appropriation protocol requires area-specialist citations before invoking any comparative frame for non-Eurasian traditions"
    never_use: []

  - native: "the adversary (category term)"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "the adversary", source: "book-internal; neutral category term" }
      - { form: "the dragon", source: "book-internal; morphological descriptor" }
      - { form: "the monstrous adversary", source: "book-internal" }
      - { form: "the chaos-figure", source: "popularising usage" }
    chosen: "'the adversary' as the default neutral category term; 'the dragon' only where the figure is visually or morphologically draconic (Leviathan, Aži Dahāka, Fáfnir, Zmey Gorynych, Typhon, etc.)"
    rationale: "Cross-chapter consistency benefits from one general term for the class of figure the champion confronts. 'The adversary' is the neutral choice and fits every case including those (Anzû the lion-headed eagle, Asag the stone-demon, Mishipeshu the underwater manitou) where 'dragon' would misrepresent the figure. 'Chaos-figure' is banned because it begs the very question the book is investigating (Ballentine 2015's critique)."
    first_mention_gloss: "the book's umbrella term for the class of figure the champion confronts in a combat myth; the term is deliberately neutral between dragon, serpent, sea-monster, stone-demon, rebel deity, and other morphologies"
    never_use: ["chaos-figure (begs the question); chaos-monster (same reason; the corpus is not uniformly 'chaos')"]

  # ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
  # METHODOLOGICAL TERMS
  # ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

  - native: "HERO SLAY SERPENT (formula)"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "HERO SLAY SERPENT", source: "Watkins 1995 (small capitals)" }
      - { form: "hero-slay-serpent formula", source: "Watkins 1995, running text" }
    chosen: "HERO SLAY SERPENT (in small capitals when naming Watkins's reconstructed formula; 'hero-slay-serpent formula' in running body prose)"
    rationale: "Small-capital convention is Watkins's own and should be preserved when naming the formula as a term of art; but body prose should not overuse the SMALL CAPS typography."
    first_mention_gloss: "Calvert Watkins's reconstructed Indo-European poetic formula (subject + verb-of-killing + serpent-object), attested in Vedic áhann áhim, Hittite kuenta... illuyanka, and elsewhere"
    never_use: []

  - native: "áhann áhim"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "áhann áhim", source: "Watkins 1995; Jamison & Brereton 2014" }
      - { form: "ahann ahim", source: "non-diacritical" }
    chosen: "áhann áhim"
    rationale: "Diacritics standard in Vedic scholarship; Watkins and Jamison-Brereton both use the accented form; the formula is the load-bearing exemplar and must be rendered as scholarship renders it."
    first_mention_gloss: "'he slew the serpent' — the Rigvedic formula (RV 1.32.1) that Watkins argues preserves the Indo-European poetic diction of combat-myth"
    never_use: []

  - native: "basic myth (Ivanov-Toporov)"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "basic myth", source: "Ivanov & Toporov 1965–74; now standard in translation" }
      - { form: "основной миф", source: "Russian original; not used in English body" }
    chosen: "'basic myth' (in quotation marks on first mention)"
    rationale: "The quotation marks flag it as a technical reconstruction whose status is contested (per Ch. 20 framing)."
    first_mention_gloss: "Ivanov and Toporov's 1965–74 reconstruction of a shared Slavic storm-god-vs-serpent narrative, drawn from folklore and toponyms, and now criticised for methodological circularity"
    never_use: []

  - native: "tier-ranking"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "tier-ranking", source: "book-internal; methods sidebar Ch. 33" }
    chosen: "tier-ranking"
    rationale: "Internal book term referring to CORE / RELATED / PROVISIONAL / CONTRASTIVE categorisation."
    first_mention_gloss: "the book's scheme for categorising chapters by the strength and type of primary attestation and by whether the chaoskampf framing is contested (CORE / RELATED / PROVISIONAL / CONTRASTIVE)"
    never_use: []

  - native: "other-than-human persons"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "other-than-human persons", source: "Hallowell 1960" }
      - { form: "non-human persons", source: "some later ethnographic writing" }
    chosen: "other-than-human persons"
    rationale: "Hallowell's exact phrase; central to the Anishinaabe (Ch. 32) and C8 chapters."
    first_mention_gloss: "A. Irving Hallowell's 1960 term for the Anishinaabe ontological category that includes animals, plants, thunder-beings, and manitouk as persons on equal standing with human persons"
    never_use: []

  - native: "Gunkel's thesis (Schöpfung und Chaos)"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "Gunkel's Chaoskampf thesis", source: "Scurlock & Beal 2013 subtitle" }
      - { form: "Gunkel's 1895 thesis", source: "standard" }
    chosen: "Gunkel's 1895 thesis"
    rationale: "Disambiguates from Gunkel's other work; the date is load-bearing (book emphasises how the term travelled for 130 years)."
    first_mention_gloss: "Hermann Gunkel's argument (1895) that Genesis 1 and Revelation 12 both preserve the demythologised remnants of a Near Eastern combat myth, of which Enūma Eliš is the paradigm"
    never_use: []

  - native: "Ballentine critique"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "Ballentine's critique", source: "Ballentine 2015; used internally" }
      - { form: "the conflict-myth re-reading", source: "Ballentine 2015's own phrase" }
    chosen: "the Ballentine critique (on first mention); thereafter 'Ballentine 2015'"
    rationale: "Short and clear; flags that this is a contemporary critical position."
    first_mention_gloss: "Debra Scoggins Ballentine's 2015 argument that 'chaos' is the wrong word for the adversaries in the combat-myth corpus, because they are presented in the ancient texts as rival sovereigns with their own retinue and power structure, not as inchoate disorder"
    never_use: []

  # ── Hebrew Bible and New Testament ──────────────────────────────────

  - native: "Yahweh"
    renderings_considered:
      - { form: "Yahweh", source: "vocalized reconstruction; Day 1985 uses this form" }
      - { form: "YHWH", source: "standard unvocalized tetragrammaton" }
      - { form: "the LORD", source: "translation convention" }
    chosen: "Yahweh"
    rationale: "The vocalised form is common enough in academic and general English that the unvocalised YHWH becomes awkward in sustained body prose; 'Yahweh' reads naturally in narrative discussion of the deity as a character. YHWH may still appear in direct quotation of sources that use the unvocalised form."
    first_mention_gloss: "the God of Israel (Hebrew tetragrammaton YHWH, vocalised Yahweh); depicted in the combat-myth passages as storm-god who defeats the sea, Leviathan, and Rahab"
    never_use: ["Jehovah"]

    rationale: "Genesis 1:2 is quoted and referenced repeatedly across Ch. 8, the introduction, and the Gunkel–Ballentine discussions; keeping tehôm as the technical term through every mention preserves the philological cognancy argument (tehôm / Tiamat) without switching registers mid-paragraph."

      - { form: "Sigurðr", source: "Old Norse philological" }
      - { form: "Siegfried", source: "Middle High German (Nibelungenlied)" }
    chosen: "Sigurd in Old-Norse contexts; Siegfried where the Nibelungenlied is under discussion"
    rationale: "Each form belongs to a distinct textual tradition."
    first_mention_gloss: "Norse hero (Middle High German Siegfried); slays the dragon Fáfnir and tastes his blood, gaining knowledge of birdspeech"

    first_mention_gloss: "Andean 'sacred power-being' (Quechua; older Spanish-derived spelling: huaca); the Huarochirí Manuscript records a landscape populated with wak'as in hierarchical and combative relationships — the pattern is wak'a-succession, not chaos-versus-order"

deviations_from_instructions:
  - note: |
      The glossary includes several language-pair entries (e.g., "Ea / Enki", "Tarḫunna / Teshub",
      "Apep / Apophis", "Thraētaona / Fereydun") where a single deity figures in two or more
      language-strata used in the book. In each case, the chosen field specifies the
      context-conditional rendering rather than a single locked form. This is a slight
      expansion of the skill's single-rendering convention, but scope.md's "translation-mediated"
      policy and cross-cultural structure make context-sensitivity unavoidable.
  - note: |
      `never_use` is kept empty or minimal for most entries (per the stage instructions
      about cross-cultural books), used only where actual cross-tradition confusion risk
      exists (e.g., "Ningirsu" for Ninurta; "Yamato no Orochi" for Yamata no Orochi;
      "Kingu" for Qingu; "Zu" for Anzû).

self_checks:
  target_count_60_120: "181 terms — above the 60–120 target. The cross-cultural scope (15+ traditions) and the need to lock transliteration choices (per scope.md §'Languages of primary sources') justify the overshoot. On user review, some single-chapter proper nouns with unambiguous transliteration could be trimmed."
  every_recurring_figure_included: "Leviathan (Chs. 8, 9, 42, 43), Aži Dahāka (Chs. 13, 37, 43), Ninurta (Chs. 1, 2, 43), Tiamat (Chs. 3, 43), maʿat/isfet (Ch. 16, 17, 36), purulli (Ch. 6, 36), HERO SLAY SERPENT (Ch. 19, 38), Chaoskampf/combat myth (every chapter) — all present"
  cross_tradition_confusion_guarded:
    - "Ninurta vs. Ningirsu"
    - "Qingu vs. Kingu (orthographic only, but flagged)"
    - "Ea vs. Enki (language-context conditional)"
    - "Zu vs. Anzû (the old misreading)"
    - "Yamato vs. Yamata (for Orochi)"
  decision_flags_elevated_to_DECIDE_GLOSSARY: true
```

---

# Skill instructions: post-human-normalize

---
name: post-human-normalize
description: Validates and normalizes any file that has been modified by a human reviewer before it becomes input for the next stage. Ensures that human edits have not broken the structural contracts that downstream stages depend on. Runs after every [HUMAN] review checkpoint.
---

# post-human-normalize

## For the human

Every time you review and edit a file — approving factcheck findings, fixing claims, resolving audit issues — there is a risk that your edits break something the next stage depends on. You might merge two claims into one paragraph, accidentally delete a marker, change a citation format, or remove a field from a YAML file. These are easy mistakes to make, and the next AI stage will silently produce wrong output rather than catching the problem.

This step is a quick automated check that catches these problems before they propagate. It runs after every human review point and validates that the edited file still meets the structural requirements for the next stage.

This step does not change the meaning of anything you wrote. It only checks structure, reformats where necessary, and flags violations that need your attention before proceeding.

## When to run

After every `[HUMAN]` checkpoint in the stage order:
- After inventory-audit human review → validates `inventory.approved.yaml`
- After intro-chapter factcheck human review → validates `chapters/00-introduction.adoc`
- After claims-factcheck human review → validates `chapters/NN-<slug>.claims.approved.adoc`
- After narrative-fidelity human review (if REVISE) → validates `chapters/NN-<slug>.adoc`
- After comparative-chapter factcheck human review → validates `comparative.adoc`
- After character-appendix factcheck human review → validates `character-appendix.adoc`

## Checks performed

### For YAML files (`inventory.approved.yaml`, factcheck outputs)
1. All required fields present per the schema defined in the producing skill.
2. No empty required fields.
3. All `source_id` values resolve to entries in `sources.yaml` registry.
4. No YAML syntax errors.

### For claims documents (`*.claims.approved.adoc`)
1. One claim per paragraph — no merged or bundled claims.
2. Every claim paragraph has a footnote citation.
3. Every evidence token (`// EVIDENCE: ...`) is syntactically valid and references a `source_id` in `sources.yaml`.
4. All markers (`[INFERENCE:]`, `[LACUNA:]`, `[RECONSTRUCTION:]`, `[VARIANT:]`) have all required sub-fields.
5. `glossary.yaml` renderings used consistently.

### For prose documents (intro, comparative, character-appendix, narrative chapters)
1. All markers syntactically valid with required sub-fields.
2. All evidence tokens syntactically valid.
3. Canonical chapter anchors present and unchanged.
4. `glossary.yaml` renderings used consistently.
5. No `never_use` glossary terms present.

## Output

The validated file, with a provenance comment block appended:
```
// Validated by post-human-normalize
// Timestamp: <UTC>
// Checks passed: <list>
// Warnings: <any non-blocking issues>
```

If any check fails, the file is returned to the human with a specific error report. The pipeline does not proceed until all checks pass.

## Completion protocol

As your very last action — after all output files are written and all self-checks pass — write a completion record. This allows the pipeline runner to verify that no stage was truncated by a timeout, rate limit, or context overflow.

**File**: `books/<book>/completions/<NN>-<stage-name>.done.yaml`

Create the `completions/` directory if it does not exist.

**Format**:
```yaml
stage: "<stage-name>"
timestamp: "<UTC ISO 8601>"
status: "completed"
agent_model: "<your model name>"
outputs:
  - file: "<relative path from book dir>"
    lines: <line count>
  # repeat for each output file
summary: "<one-line description of what was produced>"
```

**Rules**:
1. Write this file only after ALL outputs are complete and verified.
2. The `lines` count must be the actual line count of each file at the time of writing — do not estimate.
3. If you were unable to complete all outputs, write the file with `status: "partial"` and list which outputs are missing in a `missing` field.
4. Never write `status: "completed"` if any output file is missing or truncated.

## Handoff
The validated file proceeds to the next stage as defined in the stage order.

---

# Input file: chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.adoc

```
[[ch-19-ie-reflexes-claims]]
== Indo-European Reflexes of HERO SLAY SERPENT — Claims

// One fact per paragraph; chapter-draft will transform this into narrative.
// Family-chapter (related-tier) treatment: six dragon-combat sub-cases
// (Sigurd-Fáfnir, Beowulf-Grendel-and-the-dragon, Lugh-Balor, Welsh
// Red-and-White Dragon, Batraz, Vahagn-and-the-vishaps) each in its own
// === sub-section, ordered from strongest philological evidence (Sigurd)
// to most fragmentary (Vahagn). Per brief, each sub-section is 1000-1500
// words and the chapter targets ~8500 narrative words.

=== Source overview

The chapter surveys six Indo-European post-Vedic, post-Iranian, and post-Anatolian dragon-combat sub-cases that Calvert Watkins's *How to Kill a Dragon: Aspects of Indo-European Poetics* identifies as reflexes of the reconstructed Indo-European poetic formula HERO SLAY SERPENT, ordered in the present chapter from the strongest philological evidence (the Old Norse Sigurd-Fáfnir corpus) to the most fragmentary (the Armenian Vahagn material).footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml; Watkins 1995, *How to Kill a Dragon: Aspects of Indo-European Poetics*, Part V on the IE reflexes of HERO SLAY SERPENT, with ch. 42 specifically on the Old Norse Sigurd material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V; ch. 42

The chapter's principal Indo-European-poetic anchor is Calvert Watkins's *How to Kill a Dragon*, published by Oxford University Press in 1995, with chapter 42 of the volume reading the Eddic Fáfnismál diction as a direct reflex of the reconstructed IE formula and chapters in the volume's Celtic and Germanic clusters supplying the wider IE-comparative apparatus that the chapter draws on for the Lugh, Welsh, and Beowulf sub-cases.footnote:[Watkins 1995, *How to Kill a Dragon: Aspects of Indo-European Poetics*, ch. 42 (Sigurd / Fáfnismál diction); Part V (chs. on Celtic and Germanic IE-comparative material).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42; Part V

Watkins's monograph is printed-only and is cited at chapter and Part level in the present chapter, with chapter 42 the load-bearing locus for the Sigurd-Fáfnir philological argument and Part V the locus for the wider IE-formula apparatus, in line with the established citation discipline for Watkins 1995 across chapters 13, 14, 15, and 18 of the present book.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 42 and Part V (printed only; cited at chapter and Part level per the established discipline).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42; Part V

The chapter's general Indo-European comparative anchor is M. L. West's *Indo-European Poetry and Myth*, published by Oxford University Press in 2007, whose chapter 6 ("Storm and Stream") situates the Germanic, Celtic, Ossetic, and Armenian combat-witnesses within the wider Indo-European storm-god and water-dragon complex and supplies the principal cross-tradition apparatus for the chapter's Batraz and Vahagn discussions.footnote:[West 2007, *Indo-European Poetry and Myth*, ch. 6 ("Storm and Stream"), on the Indo-European storm-god / water-dragon complex with Germanic, Celtic, Ossetic, and Armenian instances.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

West 2007 is printed-only and is cited at chapter level in the present chapter, in line with the established citation discipline for West 2007 across chapters 15 and 18 of the present book.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6 (printed only; cited at chapter level per the established discipline).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The principal primary text for the Sigurd-Fáfnir sub-case is the Eddic poem *Fáfnismál*, transmitted in the Codex Regius of the Poetic Edda (Reykjavík, GKS 2365 4to, c. 1270 CE), and the chapter cites the poem in Carolyne Larrington's revised Oxford World's Classics translation of *The Poetic Edda*, published by Oxford University Press in 2014.footnote:[Larrington 2014 (trans.), *The Poetic Edda*, rev. ed., Oxford World's Classics, including *Fáfnismál*; ISBN 9780199675340; Codex Regius GKS 2365 4to as the principal manuscript witness.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál

Larrington's translation is printed-only and is cited at hymn-and-stanza level for the *Fáfnismál* (the poem has 44 stanzas in the Neckel-Kuhn numbering, broadly followed by Larrington), with stanza references reserved for the points where a specific stanza is the load-bearing locus, in line with the citation discipline applied to Larrington 2014 across chapter 18 of the present book.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* (44 stanzas in the Neckel-Kuhn numbering broadly followed by the translation); printed only; cited at hymn-and-stanza level for specific passages per the established discipline.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál stanzas 1-44

The principal primary text for the Beowulf sub-case is the Old English poem *Beowulf*, surviving in a single manuscript witness — the Nowell Codex (London, British Library, Cotton Vitellius A.xv, c. 1000 CE) — and the chapter cites the poem at line-number and episode level keyed to the standard Klaeber edition (4th edition by R. D. Fulk, Robert E. Bjork, and John D. Niles, 2008), with the specific edition pinned at glossary-lock per the brief's identifier_missing flag for the Beowulf material.footnote:[*Beowulf*, single manuscript witness London BL Cotton Vitellius A.xv (Nowell Codex), c. 1000 CE; standard reference edition Klaeber 4th ed. (Fulk, Bjork, Niles 2008) — to be pinned at glossary-lock per inventory.approved.yaml identifier_missing flag for the beowulf-dragons entry; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml special_instructions on the same flag; Watkins 1995, Part V, on the poem's IE-reflex status.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The Beowulf material is therefore cited in the present chapter at corpus level, at episode level (the Grendel episode lines 86–1250; the Grendel's mother episode lines 1251–1887; the dragon episode lines 2200–3182 in the standard Klaeber line-numbering), and at chapter / poem level rather than at sub-page granularity, with edition-pinning deferred to glossary-lock and the citations referring to "Beowulf, the dragon episode" or to "Beowulf, ll. XXX–YYY in the standard editions" per the established practice for identifier_missing material.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the deferred edition-pinning; *Beowulf* line-numbers per standard Klaeber-edition convention (4th ed. 2008); cited in this chapter at episode and corpus level; Watkins 1995, Part V, on the poem's IE-reflex status.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The principal primary text for the Lugh-Balor sub-case is the Middle Irish prose narrative *Cath Maige Tuired* ("The Battle of Mag Tuired"), transmitted in manuscripts from the 12th to the 16th century CE (principally the 16th-century Harleian MS 5280 in the British Library), with the standard critical edition Elizabeth A. Gray's *Cath Maige Tuired: The Second Battle of Mag Tuired* in the Irish Texts Society series (1982); the specific edition is pinned at glossary-lock per the brief's identifier_missing flag for the Lugh-Balor material.footnote:[*Cath Maige Tuired*, manuscripts 12th–16th c. CE (principal witness Harleian MS 5280, 16th c.); standard critical edition Gray 1982 in the Irish Texts Society series — to be pinned at glossary-lock per inventory.approved.yaml identifier_missing flag for the lugh-balor entry; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml special_instructions on the same flag.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The chapter therefore cites the *Cath Maige Tuired* material at chapter / episode level (the Second Battle of Mag Tuired sequence; the Lugh-Balor encounter episode) rather than at sub-page granularity, with edition-pinning deferred to glossary-lock per the established practice for identifier_missing material in the present book.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the deferred edition-pinning for Cath Maige Tuired; West 2007 ch. 6 on Lugh-Balor as IE warrior / dragon-slayer reflex; cited at chapter and episode level per the established discipline.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The principal primary texts for the Welsh Red-and-White Dragon sub-case are two: the Latin *Historia Brittonum* (the so-called Nennius-recension, conventionally dated c. 828 CE) and the Middle Welsh prose tale *Lludd and Llefelys*, transmitted in the *Mabinogion* manuscripts (the White Book of Rhydderch c. 1350 CE and the Red Book of Hergest c. 1382–1410 CE), with both texts cited at chapter / episode level in the present chapter and the principal scholarly anchor Daniel Ogden's *Drakōn* chapter on Celtic material.footnote:[*Historia Brittonum* c. 828 CE (Nennius recension); *Lludd and Llefelys* in the *Mabinogion*, principal manuscript witnesses White Book of Rhydderch c. 1350 CE and Red Book of Hergest c. 1382–1410 CE; Ogden 2013, *Drakōn: Dragon Myth and Serpent Cult in the Greek and Roman Worlds*, ch. on Celtic material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

Ogden 2013 is printed-only and is cited at chapter level in the present chapter, with the specific chapter-locus the volume's Celtic-material chapter; this is in line with the established citation discipline for Ogden 2013 across chapter 12 of the present book.footnote:[Ogden 2013, ch. on Celtic material (printed only; cited at chapter level per the established discipline).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

The principal primary text for the Batraz sub-case is the Ossetic Nart saga corpus, transmitted as oral epic recorded in the 19th and 20th centuries from Ossetian, Circassian, and Chechen narrators, with the principal scholarly anchor Georges Dumézil's twentieth-century reconstructions reading Batraz as the Scytho-Alanic Indo-European warrior / dragon-slayer reflex; the chapter cites the Nart material at corpus level rather than at specific-edition level per the brief's identifier_missing flag for the Batraz material.footnote:[Ossetic Nart saga corpus, 19th- and 20th-century recordings from Ossetian, Circassian, and Chechen narrators; West 2007, ch. 6, on Batraz as Scytho-Alanic IE warrior / dragon-slayer reflex following Dumézil's twentieth-century reading; cited at corpus level per inventory.approved.yaml identifier_missing flag for the batraz-nart entry.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The principal primary text for the Vahagn sub-case is Movses Khorenatsi's *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* ("History of the Armenians"), conventionally dated to the 5th to 9th century CE in successive scholarly assessments, whose book I chapter 31 supplies the standard locus for Khorenatsi's late paraphrase of an earlier Armenian hymn to Vahagn — a hymn whose text itself is lost — with the principal scholarly anchor James R. Russell's *Zoroastrianism in Armenia* (Harvard Iranian Series 5, 1987).footnote:[Movses Khorenatsi, *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* book I (with the Vahagn paraphrase at I.31); composition conventionally dated 5th–9th c. CE in successive scholarly assessments; Russell 1987, *Zoroastrianism in Armenia* (Harvard Iranian Series 5; ISBN 9780674968509), Vahagn chapter; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the fragmentary status of the Vahagn corpus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

Russell 1987 is printed-only and is cited at chapter level in the present chapter, with the specific chapter-locus the volume's Vahagn chapter; the chapter's Vahagn discussion is presented with explicit fragmentary framing throughout, since the hymn-text itself is lost and what survives is only Khorenatsi's late paraphrase.footnote:[Russell 1987, Vahagn chapter (printed only; cited at chapter level per the established discipline); per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the explicit fragmentary framing required for the Vahagn material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

The chapter's secondary survey-anchor is Neil Forsyth's *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth*, published by Princeton University Press in 1987, whose treatment of the Indo-European combat-myth supplies a wider scholarly anchor for the long-arc framing of the chapter's six sub-cases as reflexes of a single inherited combat-pattern.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth*, treatment of Indo-European combat-myth as background to the six sub-cases of the present chapter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=on IE combat-myth

The chapter is a family-chapter (related-tier) treatment, organising six Indo-European dragon-combat sub-cases by their position on a philological-evidence spectrum — from the strongest philological evidence (the Old Norse Eddic Sigurd-Fáfnir corpus where Watkins reads the diction as a direct IE-formula reflex) to the most fragmentary (the Armenian Vahagn material attested only through Khorenatsi's late paraphrase of a lost hymn) — and the six sub-cases are treated in their own clearly-bounded sub-sections that are not merged at the level of either narrative content or comparative framing.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the family-chapter framing with six sub-cases organised on a philological-evidence spectrum; Watkins 1995 ch. 42 (Sigurd as strongest philological evidence); Russell 1987 (Vahagn as most fragmentary).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42

The chapter's overall argument is that these six narratives — Sigurd's killing of Fáfnir, Beowulf's killings of Grendel and the hoard-dragon, Lugh's killing of Balor at the Second Battle of Mag Tuired, the Welsh Red-and-White Dragon combat, Batraz's combats in the Nart saga, and Vahagn's killing of the *vishaps* — are the Indo-European reflexes that the Watkins HERO SLAY SERPENT formula covers, with the chapter feeding directly into Part II C5 of the present book (the comparative chapter on the IE formula) as its main feeder.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the chapter as the main feeder to Part II C5; Watkins 1995, Part V, on the consolidated IE-reflex argument; West 2007, ch. 6, on the wider IE storm-god / water-dragon complex.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

=== Sigurd vs. Fáfnir — strongest philological evidence

The Sigurd-Fáfnir narrative is preserved principally in the Eddic poem *Fáfnismál* in the Codex Regius (GKS 2365 4to, c. 1270 CE), one of three closely-grouped poems of the Sigurd-cycle in the Codex (with *Reginsmál* preceding and *Sigrdrífumál* following in the manuscript order), and is also attested in the prose *Völsunga saga* (c. 1270 CE) and in the Middle High German *Nibelungenlied* (c. 1200 CE) as the related Germanic witness with Sigurd in the German form Siegfried.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* and the surrounding Codex Regius poems (*Reginsmál* preceding, *Sigrdrífumál* following) on Sigurd; *Völsunga saga* c. 1270 CE; *Nibelungenlied* c. 1200 CE as the related Middle High German Siegfried witness; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the related-Germanic-witness status of the Nibelungenlied.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál

Sigurd is the son of Sigmund of the Völsung lineage, raised in the household of the smith Regin after his father's death, and the *Fáfnismál* presents him as a young hero on the threshold of his first major heroic deed, the killing of the dragon Fáfnir on the heath of Gnitaheiðr.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* and the surrounding Codex Regius poems (with *Reginsmál* on Sigurd's upbringing and Regin's role); on the Völsung lineage and Gnitaheiðr setting.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Reginsmál; Fáfnismál

Fáfnir is a dwarf transformed into a dragon by his guardianship of the cursed gold of Andvari, gold that Loki had earlier extorted from the dwarf Andvari to pay a wergild to Hreidmar (Fáfnir's and Regin's father) for the killing of Otr, and the curse on the gold is the load-bearing motif that drives the Sigurd-cycle from the killing of the dragon to Sigurd's own death.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Reginsmál* and *Fáfnismál*, on the cursed gold of Andvari, Loki's extortion of the hoard, the wergild for Otr, Fáfnir's transformation into a dragon, and the curse-driven trajectory of the cycle; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the cursed-gold and Sigurd-death motifs.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Reginsmál; Fáfnismál

Regin, Fáfnir's brother and the smith who fosters Sigurd, forges the sword Gram from the fragments of Sigmund's broken sword and incites Sigurd to ambush Fáfnir on the heath, and the *Fáfnismál* opens with Sigurd at the moment of the killing-stroke, having dug a pit on the dragon's path to the water and lying in wait below.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Reginsmál* on the forging of Gram from Sigmund's sword-fragments and Regin's incitement; *Fáfnismál* opening on the pit-and-ambush mechanism.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál opening

The killing-stroke itself is delivered with the sword Gram from the pit: Sigurd thrusts upward into Fáfnir's underbelly as the dragon passes over the pit on his way to the water, and the *Fáfnismál* presents the killing-stroke as the central act around which the poem's dialogue is organised rather than as an extended combat-narrative.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* on Sigurd's upward thrust with Gram from the pit and on the structure of the poem as dialogue around the central killing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál

The post-killing dialogue between Sigurd and the dying Fáfnir occupies the bulk of the *Fáfnismál*: Fáfnir, mortally wounded, asks Sigurd's name, warns him of the curse on the gold, and supplies cosmological-and-genealogical lore in the wisdom-dialogue register that the *Vafþrúðnismál* and *Grímnismál* establish elsewhere in the Eddic corpus.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál*, on the post-killing wisdom-dialogue between Sigurd and Fáfnir, including the curse-warning and the cosmological-and-genealogical exchange; on the Eddic wisdom-dialogue register paralleled in *Vafþrúðnismál* and *Grímnismál*.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál stanzas (post-killing dialogue)

After Fáfnir's death Regin requests that Sigurd roast the dragon's heart, and when Sigurd touches the cooking heart with his finger and licks the burned blood from his finger he gains understanding of the speech of birds, and the nuthatches in the surrounding trees warn him that Regin plans to kill him for the treasure.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* on the heart-roasting, the blood-tasting, the finger-burn, the bird-speech understanding, and the nuthatches' warning; on the resulting threat from Regin.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál (heart-roasting and bird-speech)

Sigurd, warned by the birds, kills Regin in turn — beheading him with Gram — and then takes Fáfnir's hoard onto his horse Grani, with the *Fáfnismál* ending on Sigurd in possession of the cursed gold and on the way to the next stage of the cycle (his encounter with the valkyrie Sigrdrífa / Brynhild in the *Sigrdrífumál*).footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* concluding stanzas and *Sigrdrífumál* opening, on Sigurd's killing of Regin, the loading of the hoard onto Grani, and the bridge to the Sigrdrífa / Brynhild encounter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál concluding stanzas; Sigrdrífumál opening

The Sigurd-cycle's outcome is ambiguous in a way that distinguishes it from the cosmogonic and sovereignty-establishment subtypes of the combat myth: Sigurd kills the dragon and gains the hoard, but the curse on the gold drives the long-arc tragedy of the cycle (Brynhild's death, the Burgundian disasters, Sigurd's own death at the hands of Gunnar's brother Gutthorm) and the killing therefore destroys rather than secures the hero's prosperity.footnote:[Larrington 2014, on the long-arc tragedy of the Sigurd-cycle from *Fáfnismál* through *Sigrdrífumál* and the Burgundian poems to Sigurd's death; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the ambiguous-outcome reading and on the forward-link to Part II C7 (inversion problem).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Sigurd-cycle long arc

[INFERENCE: the reading of the Sigurd-cycle's outcome as ambiguous — the killing brings the hero the treasure but also the curse that will destroy him — is the chapter's editorial reading of the long-arc tragedy of the cycle, drawn from Larrington 2014 on the Codex Regius poems and from Watkins 1995 ch. 42 on the diction of the killing, and is presented in the chapter as the principal forward-link to Part II C7 of the present book (the inversion problem); the *Fáfnismál* itself does not in so many words frame the killing as destroying-rather-than-securing prosperity, but the curse-warning of Fáfnir to Sigurd and the long-arc trajectory of the surrounding Eddic poems supply the basis for the inference. | basis: Larrington 2014 on the curse-driven trajectory of the Sigurd-cycle including Fáfnir's curse-warning at *Fáfnismál*; Watkins 1995 ch. 42 on the philological diction of the killing; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the C7-inversion forward-link | risk: the framing as "ambiguous outcome" is a comparative-typological reading rather than a direct attestation, and the *Fáfnismál* presents the killing as a heroic deed in its own right within the poem's own register.]

The Sigurd-cycle is also attested in the prose *Völsunga saga* (c. 1270 CE), which gives a continuous-narrative version of the cycle including the Fáfnir-killing episode, and the prose witness is treated by Old Norse scholarship as preserving older oral-tradition material alongside the Eddic poetic witnesses; the present chapter cites the *Völsunga saga* as a parallel witness rather than as the primary text for the *Fáfnismál* episode itself.footnote:[*Völsunga saga* c. 1270 CE as continuous-narrative parallel witness to the Sigurd-cycle including the Fáfnir-killing episode; Larrington 2014 on the Eddic-and-prose witness relationship.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Sigurd-cycle parallel witnesses

The Middle High German *Nibelungenlied* (c. 1200 CE) is the related Germanic witness to the Sigurd-cycle, with Sigurd in the German form Siegfried and the dragon-killing transposed into a brief retrospective narrative episode rather than treated as a focal scene of the poem; the *Nibelungenlied* is cited in the present chapter at corpus level as the related Germanic witness rather than as a primary text for the Fáfnir-episode.footnote:[*Nibelungenlied* c. 1200 CE on Siegfried's dragon-killing in retrospective narrative; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the related-Germanic-witness status of the Nibelungenlied as parallel to the Old Norse Sigurd material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Sigurd / Siegfried Germanic parallel

In Watkins's reading in *How to Kill a Dragon* chapter 42, the Eddic *Fáfnismál* diction of Sigurd's killing is a direct reflex of the reconstructed Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT formula: the verb of killing (Old Norse *vega*, "to kill in combat"), its subject (the named hero), and its object (the named dragon) instantiate in Old Norse the same syntactic-and-semantic structure that Vedic *áhann áhim* ("he slew the serpent") instantiates in Vedic Sanskrit and that Hittite *kuenta . . . illuyanka* ("he killed the serpent") instantiates in Hittite.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 42, on the *Fáfnismál* diction of Sigurd's killing as a direct reflex of the reconstructed IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula; Vedic *áhann áhim* (RV 1.32) and Hittite *kuenta . . . illuyanka* (CTH 321) as the comparative philological exhibits of the same formula.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42

Watkins's argument in chapter 42 is that the *Fáfnismál* preserves the IE poetic formula at the level of the verbal diction of the killing-stroke itself, not merely at the level of the structural-typological pattern, and that the Norse sub-case is therefore among the strongest philological evidence in the post-Iranian, post-Vedic, post-Anatolian corpus for the reconstructed formula's status as Indo-European poetic inheritance rather than as accidental cross-tradition convergence.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 42, on the philological strength of the Norse evidence as inheritance rather than convergence; on the *Fáfnismál* diction of the killing-stroke as the load-bearing locus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42

The Sigurd-Fáfnir episode is also one of the iconographically densest in the post-Vedic IE corpus, with at least eleven Scandinavian rune-stones from the 11th and 12th centuries CE depicting Sigurd's killing of Fáfnir (the Ramsund stone in Södermanland c. 1030 CE is the principal-and-best-preserved witness), and the iconographic dossier supplements the Eddic and prose textual witnesses as a third register of attestation alongside the verbal-narrative and the philological-formulaic.footnote:[Larrington 2014 on the Sigurd-Fáfnir iconographic tradition; Ramsund stone (Södermanland c. 1030 CE) as principal Scandinavian rune-stone witness; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the iconographic dossier as a third register of attestation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Sigurd-Fáfnir iconographic dossier

[INFERENCE: the identification of the Sigurd-Fáfnir Eddic-iconographic-philological triple-witness pattern as parallel to the Thor-Jörmungandr triple-witness pattern of chapter 18 of the present volume is a comparative reading drawn from the wider Norse evidence, presented as a parallel between two Old Norse cases rather than as a feature of either case in isolation; the parallel is presented in the chapter as a comparative observation rather than as a direct claim of the *Fáfnismál* itself. | basis: Watkins 1995 ch. 42 on the *Fáfnismál*-diction strength; Larrington 2014 on the Sigurd-iconographic dossier; brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the Thor triple-witness pattern; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the back-link to Ch. 18 | risk: the parallel is typological-comparative rather than evidence of direct connection between the two cycles.]

=== Beowulf vs. Grendel and the dragon

The Old English poem *Beowulf* survives in a single manuscript witness — London, British Library, Cotton Vitellius A.xv (the Nowell Codex), c. 1000 CE — and is conventionally assigned to a composition-period bracketed somewhere in the 7th to 10th centuries CE, with the dragon-episode forming the third and final movement of the poem after the Grendel-episode and the Grendel's-mother-episode that dominate the poem's first two movements.footnote:[*Beowulf*, single-manuscript witness London BL Cotton Vitellius A.xv (Nowell Codex), c. 1000 CE; composition 7th–10th c. CE in standard scholarly bracketing; three-movement structure (Grendel / Grendel's mother / dragon) with the dragon-episode as third and final movement; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the manuscript and composition-dating conventions.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

Beowulf is a Geatish hero — a member of the Gēatas, a southern Scandinavian people — who in the poem's first movement travels to the Danish court of king Hrothgar to defend the hall Heorot from the man-eating monster Grendel, the descendant of Cain who has been killing Hrothgar's warriors at Heorot for twelve years.footnote:[*Beowulf*, lines 86–193 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering, on the Geatish identity of Beowulf, the Danish setting, Hrothgar's court at Heorot, Grendel's twelve-year depredations, and Grendel's descent from Cain.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

Beowulf kills Grendel by tearing off his arm in a wrestling-match in the dark hall of Heorot, refusing weapons because Grendel uses none, and Grendel flees mortally wounded to die in the fenland-mere where his mother also dwells; the killing-encounter is at lines 710–836 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering.footnote:[*Beowulf*, lines 710–836 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering, on the wrestling-match killing of Grendel, the arm-tearing, the weaponless contest, and Grendel's flight to the fen-mere.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The next night Grendel's mother attacks Heorot to avenge her son, and Beowulf descends into the underwater hall of the fen-mere to kill her with a giant-forged sword that he finds in the underwater hall itself; the encounter is at lines 1251–1887 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering.footnote:[*Beowulf*, lines 1251–1887 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering, on Grendel's mother's revenge-attack, Beowulf's descent into the fen-mere, the underwater-hall combat, and the giant-forged sword.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The poem's third and final movement is set fifty years after the Grendel and Grendel's-mother encounters, with Beowulf now an old king of the Gēatas, when a treasure-hoarding dragon — the *draca* — that has been sleeping under an ancient burial-mound is roused by a thief who steals a single cup from the hoard and begins flying out by night to burn the Geatish countryside in revenge; the dragon-episode is at lines 2200–3182 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering.footnote:[*Beowulf*, lines 2200–3182 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering, on the fifty-year interval, the aged-king status, the thief-and-cup motif, the burial-mound location of the hoard, the dragon's nocturnal raids, and the third-movement framing; Watkins 1995 Part V on the dragon-slaying philological diction.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

Beowulf, now an old king, takes a small band of retainers to confront the dragon at the burial-mound; in the combat the dragon's fire is too much for his shield and his retainers flee except for the young Wiglaf, who stays with the king and helps him land the killing-blow on the dragon, but Beowulf is mortally wounded by the dragon's bite and dies in Wiglaf's arms after killing the dragon.footnote:[*Beowulf*, lines 2200–3182 (the dragon-episode), with the principal narrative beats at lines 2510–2820 (the combat itself, Wiglaf's loyalty, the killing of the dragon, and Beowulf's death-wound); Watkins 1995, Part V, on the IE-reflex reading of the dragon-killing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The aged-king status of Beowulf at the dragon-episode is the principal narrative-thematic distinction between the *Beowulf* dragon-combat and the standard heroic-initiation dragon-combat of the Sigurd-Fáfnir type: Beowulf has already done his major-hero deeds in the first two movements of the poem, and the dragon-combat is end-of-life and death-bringing rather than heroic-initiatory and life-establishing.footnote:[*Beowulf*, lines 2200–2820 on the aged-king status; Watkins 1995, Part V, on the contrast between aged-king dragon-combat and heroic-initiation dragon-combat patterns; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the contrast.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The aged-king-dies-killing-the-dragon pattern of *Beowulf* is also the principal thematic parallel to the Thor-Jörmungandr pattern of chapter 18 of the present volume: in both cases the hero kills the dragon and is mortally injured in the act, and both narratives end with the hero's death after the killing-stroke; the parallel is presented in the chapter as a comparative-typological observation rather than as evidence of direct textual connection.footnote:[*Beowulf*, lines 2200–3182 on the aged-king dragon-combat and the mutual-killing outcome; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the back-link to Ch. 18 (Thor-Jörmungandr) and on the aged-champion-dies-killing-the-serpent thematic rhyme; brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the Thor pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The *Beowulf* dragon is also distinct from Grendel and Grendel's mother at the level of morphology and conceptual category: Grendel and his mother are anthropoid-monstrous descendants-of-Cain whom the poem locates in a biblical-genealogical frame, while the dragon is a *draca* in the Old English category-sense and morphologically a serpentine fire-breathing hoard-guardian for whom the poem makes no biblical-genealogical commitment.footnote:[*Beowulf* on the descendants-of-Cain framing of Grendel (lines 102–114) and on the *draca* category (lines 2200ff); Watkins 1995 Part V on the *draca* as IE-formula reflex distinct from the biblical-Cain framing of Grendel.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

In Watkins's reading in *How to Kill a Dragon* Part V, the *Beowulf* dragon-episode preserves the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT poetic diction in the verbal phrasing of the killing and in the structural-narrative pattern of the combat, with the Old English dragon-vocabulary (*draca*, *wyrm*) and the killing-vocabulary instantiating the same IE-poetic structure that the Vedic, Hittite, and Norse comparanda instantiate in their respective languages.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Part V, on the *Beowulf* dragon-episode as IE HERO SLAY SERPENT reflex in the killing-diction and the narrative-structure; on the Old English dragon-vocabulary (*draca*, *wyrm*) as IE-formula reflex.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

J. R. R. Tolkien's 1936 British Academy lecture "Beowulf: The Monsters and the Critics" argued that the dragon — and the monsters of the poem more generally — should be read as proper mythic adversaries integral to the poem's structure rather than as decorative accretions on a putatively-historical core, and the lecture was a load-bearing critical move that re-established the mythic register of the *Beowulf* monsters within twentieth-century Old English scholarship.footnote:[Tolkien, J. R. R., "Beowulf: The Monsters and the Critics", British Academy lecture 1936, on the dragon and the monsters of *Beowulf* as proper mythic adversaries; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the lecture's load-bearing-critical-move status for the chapter's Beowulf section and on the deferral of the lecture's literary-critical afterlife to the reception coda (Ch. 42).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The Tolkien 1936 lecture is mentioned in the present chapter only at the level of its load-bearing critical move (the recovery of the mythic register of the monsters), with the lecture's wider literary-critical afterlife — its influence on twentieth-century medievalism, on Tolkien's own dragon-fiction, and on the modern academic reading of the poem — explicitly deferred to the reception coda of the present book (Chapter 42), per the brief's special_instructions on the same deferral.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Tolkien-1936 mention as load-bearing critical move and on the deferral of the lecture's literary-critical afterlife to the reception coda Ch. 42; scope.md on the reception coda's chapter-placement.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The *Beowulf* dragon-episode's Indo-European-reflex reading therefore depends on two converging arguments: Watkins's philological argument that the dragon-killing diction reflects the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula, and Tolkien's structural-critical argument that the dragon is a proper mythic adversary integral to the poem; the chapter takes both arguments as load-bearing for the IE-reflex reading without committing to a wider claim about the poem's authorial-cultural position on Christian-vs-pagan inheritance.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Part V, on the philological argument; Tolkien 1936 on the structural-critical argument; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the convergence of the two arguments and on the no-authorial-commitment guardrail.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

[INFERENCE: the chapter's reading of the *Beowulf* poem as preserving an IE HERO SLAY SERPENT reflex in the dragon-episode, while consistent with Watkins 1995 Part V on the philological diction and with Tolkien 1936 on the structural-critical recovery of the mythic register of the monsters, is a comparative-philological reading rather than an unmediated feature of the poem itself; the *Beowulf*-poet's authorial-cultural position on the Christian-vs-pagan inheritance question is contested in modern scholarship and the chapter does not adjudicate the question, taking the IE-reflex reading as a poetic-formulaic observation rather than as a claim about the poet's own self-understanding. | basis: Watkins 1995 Part V on the IE-reflex philological reading; Tolkien 1936 on the mythic-register critical recovery; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the no-authorial-commitment guardrail | risk: the IE-reflex reading is comparative-philological and the chapter avoids claims about the *Beowulf*-poet's own authorial-cultural commitments.]

The principal forward-link from the *Beowulf* sub-section is to Part II C5 of the present book (the comparative chapter on the Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT formula), in which the *Beowulf* dragon-killing diction joins the Vedic, Hittite, Norse, and Italic comparanda in the consolidated IE-formula argument; the principal back-link is to chapter 18 of the present volume (the Thor-Jörmungandr chapter), with the aged-champion-dies-killing-the-serpent thematic parallel as the load-bearing comparative observation.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the C5 forward-link and the Ch18 back-link; Watkins 1995, Part V, on the consolidated IE-formula argument; brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the Thor-pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

=== Lugh vs. Balor — the Second Battle of Mag Tuired

The Lugh-Balor narrative is preserved principally in the Middle Irish prose narrative *Cath Maige Tuired* ("The Battle of Mag Tuired"), transmitted in manuscripts from the 12th to the 16th century CE and presenting the Second Battle of Mag Tuired as the decisive combat between the Tuatha Dé Danann (the Irish gods-and-ancestors) and the Fomorians (a monstrous-and-oppressive people associated with the sea and with tyranny over the Tuatha Dé).footnote:[*Cath Maige Tuired*, manuscripts 12th–16th c. CE; standard critical edition Gray 1982 (Irish Texts Society) — to be pinned at glossary-lock per inventory.approved.yaml identifier_missing flag; West 2007 ch. 6 on the Tuatha Dé Danann / Fomorians combat-axis; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Second Battle of Mag Tuired as the decisive Tuatha-Dé combat.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

Lugh is a young warrior-hero of the Tuatha Dé Danann, the son of Cian (a Tuatha Dé) and of Ethniu (the daughter of the Fomorian Balor), and his hybrid lineage — Tuatha Dé father and Fomorian mother — is the load-bearing genealogical motif of the *Cath Maige Tuired* narrative since it positions Lugh as the only figure who can kill his Fomorian grandfather Balor and end the Fomorian tyranny.footnote:[*Cath Maige Tuired* on Lugh's hybrid lineage (Cian / Ethniu / Balor) and on the kill-the-grandfather motif; West 2007 ch. 6 on the same lineage as the IE-warrior structural-genealogical pattern; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the hybrid-lineage motif.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

Balor is the king (or principal warrior) of the Fomorians and is named in the *Cath Maige Tuired* as "Balor of the Evil Eye" (Balor Birug-derc, "Balor of the piercing eye") because of his single enormous destructive eye that can incinerate any host on which it is opened, and the tactical problem of the Second Battle of Mag Tuired is the problem of how to bring the eye down before it is opened on the Tuatha Dé host.footnote:[*Cath Maige Tuired* on Balor's title "Balor of the Evil Eye" / Balor Birug-derc; on the destructive eye and the tactical-narrative problem it poses; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the evil-eye motif.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6
```
