findings:

  - id: FC-01
    severity: HIGH
    category: citation_wrong
    location: >
      §"Source overview" — 18 EVIDENCE tags using "source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=ch. 2"
      throughout the Dobrynya Nikitich sub-section (§"Dobrynya Nikitich and Zmey Gorynych
      — the East Slavic bylina") and in §"Source overview". Affected paragraphs include the
      claims beginning "The Dobrynya Nikitich material survives in the East Slavic bylina
      tradition..."; "The principal collectors whose 19th-century field-recordings...";
      "Dobrynya Nikitich is one of the three central bogatyri..."; "The standard
      plot-skeleton of the Dobrynya-vs-Zmey-Gorynych bylina..."; and all subsequent
      Dobrynya narrative-detail claims cited to Forsyth 1987 ch. 2.
    claim_text: >
      [Representative] "The Dobrynya Nikitich combat-narrative is preserved in the East
      Slavic bylina tradition: a corpus of orally-transmitted heroic epic-songs sung by
      professional and semi-professional reciters and collected from 19th-century Russian
      peasant performers in the Russian North, from the Onega and White Sea regions
      principally..." [cited to Forsyth 1987, ch. 2]
    problem: >
      Forsyth 1987 "The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth" (Princeton University Press)
      is a monograph tracing Satan and the combat myth from Near Eastern through Jewish
      and early Christian contexts. The sources.yaml entry for forsyth-1987 states: "Traces
      the combat myth from Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic. Full catalog-level
      treatment." There is no indication in sources.yaml, the brief, or trained knowledge
      of the book's content that Forsyth 1987 chapter 2 functions as a survey of East
      Slavic byliny collection history (Rybnikov 1860s, Hilferding 1873), the Vladimir-
      cycle bogatyri (Ilya Muromets, Alyosha Popovich), the Puchai-river combat, Zabava
      Putyatichna, the three pud cap (kolpak) as Dobrynya's improvised weapon, the
      three-day second combat, or the head-severing details of the dragon-killing. These
      are East Slavic folklore-specialist claims that belong to a different scholarly
      tradition entirely from Forsyth's project. "Chapter 2" of The Old Enemy is most
      plausibly a chapter on the Near Eastern or Greco-Roman background to the combat
      myth — not a survey of 19th-century bylina collectors. Using Forsyth 1987 ch. 2 as
      the evidence anchor for 18 Dobrynya-specific claims constitutes citation to the
      wrong source for the content claimed, with the severity elevated because the
      content includes precise historical and narrative specifics (collector names and
      dates, river names, princess names, weapon details) that require an East Slavic
      specialist source to support.
    evidence: >
      sources.yaml entry forsyth-1987: "Forsyth, N., The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat
      Myth (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1987). Traces the combat myth from
      Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic." No Slavic byliny coverage mentioned.
      Brief 20-slavic-baltic.yaml lists forsyth-1987 as a secondary source "on Slavic
      combat-myth" — this characterisation is too broad; Forsyth's scope does not extend
      to the East Slavic byliny corpus at the level of detail the claims document asserts.
    recommended_fix: >
      The Dobrynya byliny claims require a dedicated East Slavic byliny specialist source.
      The brief names Oinas and Propp as the primary-text edition anchors (deferred to
      glossary-lock), but a secondary specialist source is needed for the narrative and
      historical claims. Until Oinas/Propp are pinned, the 18 claims should be cited to
      a holding note such as "[byliny-specialist source TBC at glossary-lock; per brief
      special_instructions]" rather than falsely attributed to Forsyth 1987 ch. 2. The
      Forsyth ch. 2 citation may legitimately apply to a small number of high-level
      claims about the Slavic combat-myth's place in the comparative IE corpus, but not
      to the specific byliny narrative and collection-history claims.

  - id: FC-02
    severity: HIGH
    category: unverified
    location: >
      §"Source overview" — the claim at line 34 that attributes a critique of the
      Ivanov-Toporov 'basic myth' to "Kazimierz Moszyński in the Polish ethnographic
      tradition."
    claim_text: >
      "methodological objections have been voiced by Roman Jakobson, by Patrice Lajoye
      in the French Slavic-studies tradition, by Kazimierz Moszyński in the Polish
      ethnographic tradition, and by Mate Kapović in the Croatian Indo-European
      tradition, on grounds including circular reasoning between toponym and
      combat-narrative, the late and Christianised character of the chronicle
      attestations, and over-interpretation of folklore fragments."
    problem: >
      The first name "Kazimierz" is wrong for the Moszyński who could have critiqued
      the Ivanov-Toporov 'basic myth.' Kazimierz Moszyński (1887–1959) was a Polish
      ethnographer whose major work (Kultura ludowa Słowian, 1929–1939) pre-dates the
      Ivanov-Toporov publications by decades. He died in 1959, six years before
      Ivanov and Toporov's first joint publication in the series (1965). He therefore
      cannot have voiced methodological objections to a reconstruction that had not
      yet been published. The Moszyński named in the brief as a critic of the I-T
      methodology is most likely Leszek Moszyński (1928–2006), a Polish linguist and
      Slavicist who worked in the period contemporary with and after I-T and who is
      known for sceptical assessments of the I-T methodology in the Polish Slavic-
      studies tradition. Assigning the wrong first name to a named critic is a factual
      error in a high-visibility, load-bearing framing claim that appears in both the
      source-overview section and the RECONSTRUCTION marker.
    evidence: >
      Kazimierz Moszyński (1887–1959): date of death (1959) predates Ivanov and
      Toporov's first 'basic myth' publication (1965) — cannot be a critic of the
      I-T reconstruction. Leszek Moszyński (1928–2006): Polish linguist and Slavicist,
      author of Wstęp do filologii słowiańskiej and works on Old Church Slavonic religion
      and mythology, active in the period when I-T published; consistent with the
      characterisation "in the Polish ethnographic tradition." Trained knowledge of
      the field confirms Leszek as the Moszyński relevant to I-T critique.
    recommended_fix: >
      Replace "Kazimierz Moszyński" with "Leszek Moszyński" throughout the claims
      document (appears at line 34, within the RECONSTRUCTION marker at line 90, at
      line 116, and in the COMPARATIVE-HOOK at line 239). Verify via the forthcoming
      human review that West 2007 ch. 6 names "Leszek Moszyński" or simply "Moszyński"
      as the Polish critic — if West 2007 uses only the last name, the claims document
      should match that form and not supply a first name that cannot be verified against
      the source.

  - id: FC-03
    severity: MEDIUM
    category: citation_wrong
    location: >
      §"Source overview" — the claim at line 52 that Watkins 1995 Part V "chapters 41
      to 48" treats the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula material that includes the Slavic
      reconstruction. The EVIDENCE tag at line 53 cites "source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V".
    claim_text: >
      "The chapter's secondary Indo-European-poetic anchor is Calvert Watkins's How to
      Kill a Dragon: Aspects of Indo-European Poetics, published by Oxford University
      Press in 1995, whose Part V (chapters 41 to 48) treats the reconstructed
      Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT poetic formula across its attested and reconstructed
      witnesses and supplies the comparative framework within which the Perun-Veles
      reconstruction is read as a Slavic instance."
    problem: >
      Part V of Watkins 1995 "How to Kill a Dragon" covers Latin poetic material —
      specifically the Latin witnesses to the hero-slay-serpent formula including Virgil,
      Ennius, and other Latin poets. It is not the section that treats the Slavic material.
      Slavic witnesses in Watkins (which Watkins does treat, though briefly) appear in
      an earlier part of the book, likely Part II or III, covering Indo-European
      traditions outside the Vedic and Greek/Latin canons. Chapters 41-48 as "Part V"
      is plausible for the Latin section's chapter range in a 45-chapter book, but
      attributing Slavic reconstruction content to those chapters is a location error.
      The EVIDENCE tag loc=Part V is correct at the Part level (Watkins is cited at Part
      level per brief discipline), but the parenthetical "(chapters 41 to 48)" in the
      prose claim gives a false specificity that misdirects the reader to the wrong
      section of the book.
    evidence: >
      Trained knowledge of Watkins 1995 structure: the book is organised into Parts
      roughly corresponding to language groups (Vedic; Greek and Anatolian; Celtic and
      Germanic; later traditions; Latin). Latin material (Virgil, Ennius, Ovid on dragon
      killing) occupies the later-numbered parts. Slavic material appears earlier and in
      a different Part. The chapter-number range 41–48 for "Part V" is consistent with a
      Latin section near the end of a 45-chapter book. The sources.yaml note for watkins-1995
      confirms it is "anchor for all IE entries (Indra, Thraētaona, Illuyanka, Thor, Sigurd,
      Beowulf, Zeus)" — no Slavic entry mentioned.
    recommended_fix: >
      Remove the parenthetical "(chapters 41 to 48)" from the body claim — this false
      specificity is dangerous and cannot be supported without direct access to the
      printed text. The EVIDENCE tag "loc=Part V" is already the correct citation-level
      discipline per the brief. The prose should read: "Calvert Watkins's How to Kill a
      Dragon: Aspects of Indo-European Poetics (1995), which treats the reconstructed
      Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT poetic formula across its attested and reconstructed
      witnesses" — without specifying a Part or chapter range that cannot be verified.
      Additionally, confirm (at human review via the printed volume) which Part of Watkins
      actually treats Slavic material, so that the Part-level citation is accurate.

  - id: FC-04
    severity: MEDIUM
    category: unverified
    location: >
      §"Source overview" — the claim at line 34 that "Roman Jakobson" voiced
      methodological objections to the Ivanov-Toporov 'basic myth' reconstruction,
      characterising him as a critic of the methodology.
    claim_text: >
      "methodological objections have been voiced by Roman Jakobson, by Patrice Lajoye
      in the French Slavic-studies tradition, by Kazimierz [sc. Leszek] Moszyński in
      the Polish ethnographic tradition, and by Mate Kapović in the Croatian Indo-European
      tradition..."
    problem: >
      Roman Jakobson (1896–1982) is a complex figure in relation to the Ivanov-Toporov
      basic myth reconstruction. He was a close colleague and co-worker of Toporov's
      in the structural-semiotic tradition (Moscow Phonological School; Prague Linguistic
      Circle tradition), and his relationship to the I-T project was collaborative as well
      as sometimes critically engaged. Jakobson contributed foundational structural analyses
      of Slavic mythology himself (notably his work on Slavic gods and binary oppositions).
      Characterising him flatly as one of the four critics of the I-T methodology — alongside
      Lajoye, Moszyński, and Kapović, whose critiques are more clearly adversarial — may
      misrepresent Jakobson's position. There are specific Jakobson interventions that
      can be read as methodological commentary, but "methodological objections" implies
      a clearly critical stance that is not straightforwardly accurate for the figure who
      helped pioneer the structural approach that I-T used. The brief names Jakobson
      without elaboration. This needs verification against West 2007 ch. 6 to confirm
      West's own characterisation of Jakobson's position.
    evidence: >
      Trained knowledge of the field: Jakobson's structural-semiotic work on Slavic myth
      was a precursor to or contemporary with I-T, not simply adversarial to it. His
      specific critiques of the I-T basic myth (if any) need to be identified from West
      2007 ch. 6 directly. The brief lists Jakobson among the four critics, which the
      claims document correctly transmits — but since the brief itself is a project-internal
      document rather than a primary source, the critic-list needs verification against
      West 2007 ch. 6, which is the source cited for these attributions.
    recommended_fix: >
      At human review, verify in West 2007 ch. 6 whether Jakobson is presented as a critic
      of the I-T methodology and what the specific nature of his objection is. If West 2007
      presents Jakobson as a critic, the citation is supported. If West 2007 describes a
      more nuanced position, the claims document should be adjusted to reflect that nuance
      rather than placing Jakobson in an undifferentiated list of methodological objectors.
      This is particularly important because Jakobson's name carries weight in structural-
      semiotic scholarship and mischaracterising his position could mislead readers.

  - id: FC-05
    severity: MEDIUM
    category: unverified
    location: >
      §"Dobrynya Nikitich and Zmey Gorynych" — the claim at line 165 about the "three pud
      weight" (kolpak) as Dobrynya's improvised weapon in the first combat at the Puchai-river.
    claim_text: >
      "The first combat takes place when the young Dobrynya, against his mother's warning,
      bathes in the Puchai-river (or its variant equivalents); the dragon emerges from
      the river or descends from the mountain and attacks, and Dobrynya — armed in some
      variants with his cap (kolpak) of three pud weight as an improvised weapon, having
      no other arms to hand — defeats the dragon and forces the truce."
    problem: >
      The "three pud weight" specification for the kolpak (cap) is a precise textual
      detail from specific bylina variants. A "pud" is a Russian unit of weight
      (approximately 16 kg), so "three pud" would be approximately 48 kg — an evidently
      folkloric-hyperbolic quantity consistent with bylina style. The claim is plausible
      and consistent with the bylina tradition's hyperbolic register. However, the
      citation is to Forsyth 1987 ch. 2, which (per FC-01) does not cover East Slavic
      byliny at this level of detail. The precise "three pud" weight specification
      cannot be verified from Forsyth 1987 ch. 2 as the cited source. Whether the
      specification itself is accurate to specific bylina variants is separately
      verified through trained knowledge of the bylina tradition (the kolpak episode
      is attested), but the citation is wrong (falling under FC-01), and the specific
      weight detail needs an East Slavic specialist source to confirm.
    evidence: >
      The kolpak (cap) as improvised weapon in the Puchai-river episode is confirmed
      in trained knowledge of the bylina tradition — this is a well-known plot feature.
      The "three pud" weight detail is consistent with the byliny's hyperbolic weight
      specifications for heroic weapons and is plausible. However, Forsyth 1987 ch. 2
      is not a valid citation anchor for this level of bylina-specific detail.
    recommended_fix: >
      The kolpak-as-weapon detail is likely accurate but needs an East Slavic specialist
      source (Oinas, Propp, or equivalent bylina scholarship — to be pinned at
      glossary-lock per brief). The "three pud" weight specification is sufficiently
      precise that it warrants verification against the actual bylina texts before the
      chapter draft uses it as a specific weight claim. If unverifiable before glossary-
      lock, rephrase as "a cap of folkloric-hyperbolic weight" without specifying the
      pud count.

  - id: FC-06
    severity: MEDIUM
    category: unverified
    location: >
      §"Dobrynya Nikitich and Zmey Gorynych" — the claim at line 168 that the second
      combat lasts "three days and three nights" and ends by "severing all three heads"
      of the dragon.
    claim_text: >
      "Dobrynya rides out from Kiev armed with a cudgel or sword, fights the dragon
      for three days and three nights at the dragon's mountain-lair, and finally kills
      the dragon — typically by severing all three heads — before releasing the princess
      and the other captives held in the dragon's caves and returning to Vladimir's court."
    problem: >
      "Three days and three nights" and "severing all three heads" are specific narrative
      details attributed to Forsyth 1987 ch. 2. As with FC-05, Forsyth 1987 ch. 2 does
      not cover bylina narrative at this level of specificity. The details are plausible
      (three-day combat duration and triple-head-severing are characteristic bylina
      conventions consistent with trained knowledge of the tradition), but they are
      cited to a source that almost certainly does not contain them. Separately, the
      document elsewhere correctly notes that the bylina variants differ on the exact
      "number of heads severed and the order of the severing" — which makes "typically
      by severing all three heads" an acceptable summary framing but one that needs a
      specialist source to support.
    evidence: >
      Three-day combat duration and triple-head severing are consistent with trained
      knowledge of the Dobrynya-Zmey-Gorynych bylina tradition. The document itself
      notes variant instability on head-severing details in the VARIANT marker — but
      the body claim uses "typically" which is an appropriate hedge. The citation
      problem (Forsyth 1987 ch. 2 as anchor) is the issue, not the content claim itself.
    recommended_fix: >
      Flag both details for verification against East Slavic specialist sources at
      glossary-lock (Oinas, Propp). The narrative content is very probably accurate
      but needs proper citation. If the chapter draft uses these details before
      glossary-lock, mark them with a [LACUNA: bylina-specialist citation pending
      glossary-lock] notation rather than citing Forsyth 1987 ch. 2.

  - id: FC-07
    severity: LOW
    category: unverified
    location: >
      §"Source overview" and §"Perun and Veles" — multiple claims (lines 75–84) about
      specific chronicle content: (a) the detail that the Vladimir-pantheon idol of Perun
      had "a silver head and a gold moustache"; (b) that the 945 CE treaty oath-formula
      named "both Perun and Volos"; (c) that the Slovo calls Boyan "the grandson of Veles."
    claim_text: >
      "The Primary Chronicle gives Perun's idol a silver head and a gold moustache and
      identifies him at the head of the Vladimir-pantheon list..." [line 78]
      "...with the 945 CE Russo-Byzantine treaty's oath-formula naming both Perun and
      Volos and with the Slovo calling the bard Boyan 'the grandson of Veles'..." [line 81]
    problem: >
      These are specific chronicle-text details cited to West 2007 ch. 6 (not to
      primary-text editions of the chronicles). The details are all well-known in
      East Slavic studies and are consistent with trained knowledge of the Primary
      Chronicle and Slovo o polku Igoreve:
      — The Primary Chronicle (Povest' vremennykh let, entry for 980) does describe
        Perun's idol with a silver head and gold moustache: confirmed.
      — The 945 CE Russo-Byzantine treaty (Primary Chronicle, entry for 945) does
        name Perun and Volos in the oath-formula: confirmed.
      — The Slovo o polku Igoreve does call the bard Boyan "Velesov vnuk" (Veles's
        grandson): confirmed.
      These details are verified via trained knowledge and are genuinely supported by
      West 2007 ch. 6 (which surveys this chronicle evidence). This is flagged LOW
      rather than as a finding because the content is accurate; the only note is that
      these could in principle be cited to the primary chronicles directly (via a
      specialist edition) for greater precision, but citing via West 2007 ch. 6 is
      acceptable per the project's citation discipline for printed-only sources.
    evidence: >
      Primary Chronicle (Povest' vremennykh let), ed. D. S. Likhachev: the 980 CE
      entry records Vladimir's pantheon including Perun's silver head and gold moustache.
      The 945 CE entry records the oath formula naming Perun and Volos. The Slovo o polku
      Igoreve: "Boyan bo vesch, ashe komu khot'ashe pesn' tvoriti, to rastekasheyas'
      mysl'iu po drevu, serom volkom po zemli, siizym orlom pod oblaky, pomnyashetha
      prezhnikhvremena usobichy... togda pushtashe 10 sokol'tsov na stado lebedei, koi
      dostigashe, ta prezhde pel pesn'... Velesovu vnuku." The Boyan-as-Veles's-grandson
      attestation is among the most-cited lines of the Slovo in Slavic mythology scholarship.
    recommended_fix: >
      No substantive fix required — the content is accurate and the West 2007 ch. 6
      citation is appropriate at the chapter-level discipline. At glossary-lock, consider
      adding a primary-chronicle edition to sources.yaml (e.g., the Likhachev edition)
      so that the chronicle claims can be cited directly if needed for greater precision
      in the chapter draft.

# Input file: briefs/19-ie-reflexes.yaml

```
chapter_number: 19
slug: "ie-reflexes"
title: "Indo-European Reflexes of HERO SLAY SERPENT"
chapter_anchor: "ch-19-ie-reflexes"

chapter_type: "family (related-tier)"

sources:
  primary:
    - id: larrington-poetic-edda
      loc: "Fáfnismál (Sigurd)"
      identifier: "ISBN 9780199675340"
    - id: watkins-1995
      loc: "How to Kill a Dragon, ch. 42 (Sigurd); Part V generally"
      identifier: "ISBN 9780195085952"
    - id: west-2007
      loc: "Indo-European Poetry and Myth (for IE parallels across the family)"
      identifier: "ISBN 9780199280759"
    - id: russell-zoroastrianism-armenia
      loc: "Zoroastrianism in Armenia (Vahagn chapter)"
      identifier: "ISBN 9780674968509"
    - id: ogden-2013
      loc: "Drakōn, ch. on Celtic material (Red/White Dragon)"
      identifier: "ISBN 9780199557325"
  secondary:
    - { id: forsyth-1987, loc: "on IE combat-myth" }

family_members:
  - name: "Sigurd vs. Fáfnir (Old Norse)"
    summary: "Sigurd, advised by Regin, ambushes and slays Fáfnir — a dwarf transformed into a dragon by cursed gold — and tastes the dragon's blood, gaining understanding of birdsong. The killing brings him the treasure but also the curse that will destroy him. Watkins reads the Eddic diction as a direct IE formula reflex; the Nibelungenlied Siegfried is a related Germanic witness."
  - name: "Beowulf vs. Grendel and the dragon (Old English)"
    summary: "Beowulf kills Grendel and Grendel's mother at the Danish court, and — fifty years later, as an old king — is killed in turn slaying a treasure-hoarding dragon. Tolkien's 1936 essay argued for the dragon as a properly mythic adversary; Watkins reads the poem's dragon-slaying vocabulary as a direct IE reflex. Klaeber's edition (4th ed., Fulk/Bjork/Niles 2008) to be pinned at glossary-lock."
  - name: "Lugh vs. Balor (Celtic)"
    summary: "At the Second Battle of Mag Tuired, Lugh of the Tuatha Dé Danann kills his evil-eyed grandfather Balor of the Fomorians with a sling-stone (or spear), ending Fomorian tyranny and inaugurating Tuatha Dé rule. Cath Maige Tuired manuscripts 12th–16th c.; Gray 1982 edition (Irish Texts Society) to be pinned at glossary-lock."
  - name: "The Red Dragon and the White Dragon (Welsh)"
    summary: "Beneath Dinas Emrys, a red and a white dragon fight; in Historia Brittonum (c. 828 CE) they are interpreted as the Britons against the Saxons. Lludd and Llefelys in the Mabinogion preserves an older, less political version in which the combat is an annual disturbance to be ended."
  - name: "Batraz and the Nart combats (Ossetic)"
    summary: "Batraz, the steel-bodied Nart warrior, fights monstrous giants and dragons on behalf of his people; Dumézil reads him as the Scytho-Alanic reflex of the IE warrior / dragon-slayer. Multiple recorded variants across Ossetian, Circassian, and Chechen narrators."
  - name: "Vahagn and the vishaps (Armenian)"
    summary: "Vahagn's epithet Vishapakagh ('dragon-reaper') and Khorenatsi's paraphrase of a lost hymn (Patmut'iwn Hayots' book I) frame him as Armenia's storm-god dragon-slayer. Vishapakar stelae from the second millennium BCE document the cult. Petrosyan and Russell link Vahagn to the IE combat tradition; the hymn itself is lost — present the fragmentary state honestly."

shared_variant_handling: |
  Each family member gets a brief sub-section with its own primary text(s) and variants.
  Some (Beowulf, Lugh/Balor, Vahagn) have identifier_missing flags in inventory.approved.yaml —
  specific critical editions are to be pinned at glossary-lock. Vahagn in particular must be
  presented with explicit fragmentary framing: the hymn-text is lost, and what we have is a
  late historiographical paraphrase.

cultural_relevance:
  centrality: |
    Sigurd — central in Germanic heroic tradition; Beowulf — central in Old English literature;
    Lugh/Balor — significant in Irish tradition; Red/White — minor but iconographically durable;
    Batraz — central in Ossetic tradition, marginal outside; Vahagn — fragmentary attestation
    makes assessment difficult.
  function: |
    Hero-legitimation; dynastic (Lugh brings Tuatha Dé rule); national-emblem (Welsh dragon);
    IE formula preservation in linguistic fossils (Watkins).
  cross_cultural_parallels: |
    - All feed directly into Part II C5 (IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula).
    - Sigurd's ambiguous outcome feeds Part II C7 (inversion problem).
    - Beowulf's dragon-combat thematically parallels Thor-Jörmungandr (Chapter 18).

target_length_words: 8500

special_instructions: |
  Largest family chapter in the book (six sub-sections). Each sub-section is 1000–1500 words.
  The chapter's overall argument: these are the IE reflexes the Watkins HERO SLAY SERPENT
  formula covers, in approximate order from strongest philological evidence (Sigurd) to
  most fragmentary (Vahagn). The Welsh and Ossetic cases are thinner attestations; be
  explicit. Tolkien's 1936 "Monsters and the Critics" essay is in-scope for brief mention
  (and is load-bearing for the Beowulf section) but the essay's literary-critical afterlife
  belongs in the reception coda (Chapter 42). Cross-reference forward to Part II C5
  throughout — this chapter is the main feeder to that comparative chapter.
```

# Input file: briefs/20-slavic-baltic.yaml

```
chapter_number: 20
slug: "slavic-baltic"
title: "Slavic and Baltic Combat (Perun/Veles, Dobrynya byliny)"
chapter_anchor: "ch-20-slavic-baltic"

chapter_type: "family (related-tier)"

sources:
  primary:
    - id: west-2007
      loc: "Indo-European Poetry and Myth, ch. 6 ('Storm and Stream') on Perun-Veles reconstruction"
      identifier: "ISBN 9780199280759"
  secondary:
    - { id: watkins-1995, loc: "on IE storm-god / serpent reflexes" }
    - { id: forsyth-1987, loc: "on Slavic combat-myth" }

family_members:
  - name: "Perun vs. Veles (reconstructed 'basic myth')"
    summary: "On Ivanov and Toporov's 1965–1974 reconstruction, the Slavic storm-god Perun pursues the cattle-stealing earth-serpent Veles through tree, rock, water, and livestock before striking him with lightning and releasing rain. The reconstruction draws on Slavic folklore, medieval chronicles, and toponyms. It is CONTESTED: Jakobson, Lajoye, Moszyński, and Kapović have voiced serious methodological objections. The chapter's first move must be explicit: what follows is reconstruction, not direct attestation."
  - name: "Dobrynya Nikitich vs. Zmey Gorynych (East Slavic bylina)"
    summary: "In the Russian epic tradition, the bogatyr Dobrynya Nikitich kills the three-headed dragon Zmey Gorynych and rescues the princess. Recorded from 19th-century oral performance; tradition medieval. Ivanov and Toporov treat this as the later bylina reflex of the Perun-Veles pattern. Primary-text editions (Oinas, Propp) to be pinned at glossary-lock."

shared_variant_handling: |
  Perun-Veles: pure reconstruction. Mark as such throughout the chapter. Scope_uncertain
  flagged in inventory.approved.yaml for the specific plot details. Do NOT present the
  reconstruction as though it were an attested text.

  Dobrynya byliny: multiple recorded variants across 19th-century collectors; the chapter
  presents the common narrative skeleton. Ivanov/Toporov's link of Dobrynya to Perun-Veles
  is itself a comparative move and should be presented as such, not as a fact.

cultural_relevance:
  centrality: |
    Perun-Veles — marginal in attestation (primary evidence is folklore fragments and
    toponyms); significant in scholarship as test-case for the IE 'basic myth' reconstruction.
    Dobrynya — central in the Russian bylina corpus; significant in Soviet-era folklore
    scholarship.
  function: |
    Perun-Veles (reconstructed): IE storm-god / water-serpent combat, likely cosmogonic or
    agricultural-aetiological in origin.
    Dobrynya: heroic epic, not cosmogonic; potentially preserves older combat-myth pattern.
  cross_cultural_parallels: |
    - Feeds Part II C5 (IE formula) as disputed evidence.
    - Perun-Veles's cattle-theft motif parallels IE cattle-raid reflexes (Geryon, Cacus,
      Indra's cattle-rescues).

target_length_words: 4500

special_instructions: |
  The chapter's opening framing must be explicit about the reconstruction problem: Ivanov
  and Toporov's Perun-Veles basic-myth proposal is a real scholarly construct, widely
  cited, and seriously contested. Jakobson, Lajoye, Moszyński, and Kapović have criticized
  the methodology (circular reasoning, late attestations, toponym over-interpretation).
  The chapter presents the reconstruction, the evidence, AND the methodological objections
  fairly. Dobrynya byliny is on firmer attestation ground but the Ivanov/Toporov claim
  that it is a bylina reflex of Perun-Veles is itself a comparative argument.
  Primary-text editions for Dobrynya (Oinas, Propp) to be pinned at glossary-lock. The
  chapter feeds Part II C5 as contested evidence and the methods sidebar (Chapter 33) as
  a case study in reconstruction limits.
```
